End of Rand's Slide Could Be Near --- With the South African Currency at Four-Year Lows, Some Investors Say It Can't Get Much Weaker
Currency Trading
End of Rand's Slide Could Be Near --- With the South African Currency at Four-Year Lows, Some
Investors Say It Can't Get Much Weaker
By Patrick McGroarty and Jessica Mead
773 words
13 March 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
JOHANNESBURG -- In recent weeks, the South African rand has been a one-way bet: sell.
But as the currency limps along at four-year lows, some investors are starting to wonder whether it is set to
stabilize.
On Tuesday, the rand tumbled after fresh current-account data helped underline the toll that strikes have
taken on the country's mining exports, and how expensive imports have become as the rand has weakened.
The dollar rose above 9.17 rand, after starting the week at about 9.07 rand. Central bank governor Gill
Marcus said last week the currency's drubbing was "overdone," but she isn't expected to intervene to stop it.
With weak exports, labor-market stresses and only a slim chance of central-bank action, market participants
say it is hard to see a trigger for a turnaround. But some say it also could be difficult for rand bears to keep
hammering the currency.
"It would be brave to sell the rand from here -- the currency is already 40% weaker than its high point of
2011," said Paul McNamara, an investment director at GAM in London who manages $9.5 billion in
emerging-market assets.
Mr. McNamara said that at some point in the coming months, the weaker rand will dent imports, supporting
the domestic economy and helping to boost the currency.
For now, traders' willingness to sell the rand appears to be entrenched. Tuesday's slide, for example, came
even though the current-account data was backward-looking, to the last three months of 2012. The data
showed South Africa's current-account deficit -- which includes its trade gap -- narrowed to 6.5% of gross
domestic product. That was only slightly wider than economists had forecast, but was overshadowed by an
upward adjustment to the country's third-quarter figure to 6.8% from a previously reported 6.4%.
"It's a significant deficit, but it's already in the price. And this is data that was never going to be strong. So the
fall in the rand [Tuesday] shows you that people really want to [sell], and that may last for a while," said
Bartosz Pawlowski, a currencies strategist at BNP Paribas in London.
Still, some market participants said the extent of the currency's recent decline has limited how much more it
can fall.
"Given where we have got to, that positioning is biased [against the] rand and that the central bank has
indicated it is uncomfortable with a weaker currency, we could see some stabilization in the short term," said
Nima Tayebi, an emerging-markets portfolio manager at J.P. Morgan Asset Management in London, which
runs $112 billion in foreign-exchange exposure.
"A lot of bad news has been priced in," said Binqi Liu a portfolio manager at HSBC Global Asset Management
in New York.
Some believe the central bank could take action, perhaps by selling dollars to help reverse the tide. "There
won't be any [interest] rate hikes, but the central bank will need to start using its reserves," said Wike
Groenenberg, head of emerging markets strategy at Citigroup in London.
But with no foreign-currency debt, the rationale for supporting the currency is weak. The central bank also
has limited firepower: The bank's gold and foreign exchange reserves stood at just $50.4 billion in February,
and Ms. Marcus has said repeatedly that she won't intervene to prop up the rand. A spokeswoman for the
bank didn't respond to requests seeking comment.

--- Page 116 ---
Page 116 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The rand has been on an extended slide since August, when police shot and killed 34 miners on strike at a
platinum mine outside Johannesburg. The production of exports and inward investment have shrunk since
then, hurting the currency.
The slide has continued in 2013, with the rand shedding more than 8% of its value against the dollar.
David Hauner, head of emerging market foreign-exchange strategy at Bank of AmericaMerrill Lynch in
London, said the dollar could reach as high as 10 rand, though that isn't his main expectation.
"I think from here we will get another couple of months where there's no new good news, and the currency
keeps on falling," Mr. Hauner said. "But then I think that currency weakness will help to correct the trade
balance from around May, with the pace of imports slowing."

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Is Anything Cheap? --- A four-year bull market has sent the Dow to a record high as other asset classes surge; But a...
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Is Anything Cheap? --- A four-year bull market has sent the Dow to a record
high as other asset classes surge; But a few bargains lurk for patient investors
By Jason Zweig, Joe Light and Liam Pleven
1,915 words
9 March 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
The annual expenses of iShares Core S&P Total U.S. Stock Market ETF are $7 per $10,000 invested.
Saturday's Weekend Investor cover article incorrectly said the expenses are $7 per $1,000 invested.
(WSJ March 12, 2013)
Subscribe to WSJ: http://online.wsj.com?mod=djnwires
(END)
Nearly four years to the day after the Dow Jones Industrial Average hit its bear market low, it reached a
record high this week.
Don't expect another 146% surge soon -- in stocks or any other major asset class.
The Dow's rally is only one piece of a much bigger puzzle. All manner of assets, from government and
corporate bonds to oil and other commodities, have posted big gains since the grim days of the 2008-09
financial crisis. The rising tide has been lifted partly by the slowly recovering U.S. economy but, more
important, by the monetary policy of the Federal Reserve, which has flooded the markets with cheap
money.
The problem: All that monetary stimulus has left almost everything pricey by historical examples.
"Across markets, there usually are plenty of opportunities to buy things cheap," says veteran portfolio
manager Jean-Marie Eveillard of First Eagle Investment Management in New York. "Today there are
relatively few, here and there, mainly in stocks."
That doesn't mean there aren't chances to make money for smart, patient investors. Here is how to play
stocks, bonds and commodities now:
Stocks
By one measure, U.S. stocks look overvalued. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, a broader measure of
large stocks than the Dow, is selling at 22.7 times the total earnings of the companies in the index, averaged
over the past 10 years and adjusted for inflation, according to data compiled by Robert Shiller, an economist
at Yale University. That is considerably pricier than the average multiple of roughly 16 times long-term
adjusted earnings since the data began in 1871.
But how relevant today are stock valuations from the days of President Rutherford B. Hayes? Probably not
very. If you instead count only the past half-century's worth of data, stocks have traded at an average of
around 19.5 times adjusted earnings, making today's stock market merely a bit on the high side. And it is
nowhere near the five-alarm levels of 44 times adjusted earnings in early 2000.
By a simpler measure, the stock market is moderately cheap. The S&P 500 is priced at 15.9 times operating
earnings, versus 28.4 times in 2000 and the long-term average of 18.8 times, according to senior index
analyst Howard Silverblatt of S&P Dow Jones Indices.
The simplest and cheapest way to invest in U.S. stocks is through an index mutual fund or exchange-traded
fund that captures the entire market at low cost, like the iShares Core S&P Total U.S. Stock Market ETF,
which holds 1,502 stocks at just 0.07% in annual expenses, or $7 for every $1,000 invested.

--- Page 121 ---
Page 121 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
If record highs make you nervous, consider funds with cheaper or less-risky portfolios. The Vanguard Value
ETF charges 0.1% in annual expenses to hold stocks trading at an average of 1.6 times their book value, a
basic measure of corporate net worth. That is about 25% less than the market as a whole. Likewise, the
PowerShares S&P 500 Low Volatility ETF, which charges 0.25% a year, favors stocks that tend to lose less
money than average during market declines.
For better returns, you should look overseas, especially -- if you have a tolerance for bad news -- to Europe
and Japan. "There is absolute chaos in Europe," says Albert Edwards, a global strategist at Societe Generale
in London. "But you can only buy stocks absolutely cheap when there is absolute chaos."
By the same measure of adjusted long-term earnings that shows the U.S. as moderately expensive,
European stocks are unusually attractive, says Guillermo Felices, an asset-allocation strategist at Barclays.
Using this yardstick, seven of the world's 10 cheapest markets are in Europe, including Ireland, Italy, France
and the United Kingdom -- all trading recently at less than 15 times the average of their long-term earnings
after inflation, at least 25% less expensive than the U.S. market. Japan is moderately cheap, at 19 times
long-term adjusted earnings.
Investors can get direct, low-cost exposure to these markets through an ETF like iShares Europe 350 Index
or Vanguard MSCI Europe, which are concentrated in the cheapest markets, as well as iShares MSCI Japan
or SPDR Russell/Nomura PRIME Japan. All have annual expenses of 0.6% or less.
Many U.S. investors instead have been racing to invest in emerging markets like China, India and Latin
America, which are no longer as cheap as they were in 2008 and 2009. Stocks in Latin America, for instance,
are trading at 15.9 times recent earnings, according to MSCI, the index research firm -- a smidgen more
expensive than U.S. stocks, even though Latin America historically has been a riskier place to invest.
"The picture is quite mixed" for emerging markets, Mr. Felices says, so investors should bear in mind that
these stocks are no longer an unambiguous bargain. Rather than try to guess the winners and losers, invest
patiently in a broadly diversified ETF like iShares Core MSCI Emerging Markets or Vanguard FTSE Emerging
Markets, each with annual expenses of 0.18%.
Bonds
Bond prices have risen for the better part of three decades, leaving few opportunities for bargains and
analysts speculating about an impending pop of the "bond bubble."
The problem is that those experts have been saying the same thing for years, and the bond market has kept
chugging along. Yields, which move in the opposite direction of prices, have plunged. At 2.06%, the yield on
the 10-year Treasury is down nearly 14 percentage points from the start of the rally in 1981.
Yet there are several factors supporting high bond prices in the short-term, says David Ader, head of
government bond strategy at CRT Capital Group, an institutional broker-dealer. Among them: tepid economic
growth world-wide, which encourages investors to look to Treasurys for safety, and continued bond buying by
the Federal Reserve.
On the other hand, positive economic data and a strong stock market have tempered the kind of fear that
would push yields even lower, he says. That could make for a sideways market in coming months that
presents opportunities only for the most skilled traders able to jump in and out at the right time.
Longer term, all bonds are subject to two main risks: rising interest rates, which hurt prices, and a
deterioration in credit quality.
Nowadays, with rates so low, investors should focus on places where the market is paying high enough yields
to compensate for defaults, says Kathleen Gaffney, portfolio manager of the Eaton Vance Bond Fund, which
launched at the end of January.
That knocks out long-term government and corporate bonds, which have seen huge price increases and are
highly vulnerable to rising rates.
One opportunity that carries less interest-rate risk: "floating-rate" or "leveraged" loans, Ms. Gaffney says.
Leveraged loans are adjustable-rate bank loans made to companies typically rated below investment grade.
Like high-yield bonds, leveraged loans can tank during shaky times. The S&P/LSTA U.S. Leveraged Loan
100 index dropped 28% in 2008, for example. But the loans hold appeal now because their rates are able to
move up and down with interest rates. If a company defaults, the loans also have among the highest claims
to company assets.

--- Page 122 ---
Page 122 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The trade-off for the protection is slightly lower yields. For example, the PowerShares Senior Loan Portfolio
ETF, which tracks the S&P/LSTA U.S. Leveraged Loan 100 index, carries a 0.66% annual expense ratio,
yields 4.2% and should be little affected by rising rates.
By comparison, the iShares High Yield Corporate Bond ETF, which follows a high-yield bond index, yields
5.2% and has a 0.5% expense ratio. But it has a "duration" of four years, meaning its price would drop about
4% if interest rates were to rise one percentage point.
Another leveraged-loan fund worth a look, according to Morningstar: The Eaton Vance Floating-Rate mutual
fund, whose A share class charges 1.02% annually and yields 4.2%.
Gold and Commodities
Gold is a glittering example of an asset propelled higher by Fed policies, with prices up more than fourfold
over the past 10 years. But bullion has tumbled 12% since October as investors rekindled their romance with
stocks, the yellow metal's traditional nemesis.
Some analysts think further drops are in store. Yet there also are lingering threats to the financial markets,
such as a serious flare-up of euro-zone debt woes or a geopolitical crisis, that could hammer stocks again --
and bring investors back to bullion in a hurry.
Traders inclined to buy dips should be ready to get out as quickly as they dive in, which make bullion-backed
ETFs such as SPDR Gold Trust, which carries a 0.4% annual expense ratio, and iShares Gold Trust, which
charges 0.25% in expenses, good vehicles.
Other commodities also have taken a breather of late. The Dow Jones-UBS Commodity index, which tracks
22 futures contracts from unleaded gasoline to soybean meal, has fallen 2.4% since the end of 2011, even as
stocks and bonds continued their postcrisis rallies.
Looming questions about future growth in emerging markets such as China also weaken the bull case for
some of those raw materials, given the role rising demand played in their rally over the past decade.
"The bias is more to the downside than the upside," says Peter Rup, managing director at Artemis Wealth
Advisors.
So investors need to be selective. For instance, U.S. farmers are expected to plant a record-large corn crop
this year, which could ease a tight market for the grain. Prices are down 13% from last year's record high and
2% since the end of January.
But that could prove a temporary respite. Stockpiles are low because of last year's devastating drought, and
soil remains dry in much of the Midwest, says Nick Higgins, a commodity analyst at Rabobank. "We think
prices will rise," he said in an email.
One potential catalyst in the months ahead: signs that the U.S. is in for another hot, dry summer, which could
push up prices for corn and other row crops. That could boost the iPath Dow Jones-UBS Commodity Index
Total Return exchange-traded note, which carries a 0.75% expense ratio. Corn, soybeans, wheat and related
products make up nearly a quarter of the underlying index.
Traders seeking a more targeted wager could buy shares in the Teucrium Corn ETF, which holds corn futures
and charges annual expenses of 2.55%. The fund's shares rose 41% between mid-June and mid-July last
year, as days passed without significant rain, imperiling crops.
The caveat? If the weather looks ideal for a bumper crop, traders might need to trim risk fast.

Careers: Can Kids Be Persuaded to Play With Rocks for a Living?
Careers: Can Kids Be Persuaded to Play With Rocks for a Living?
By John W. Miller
498 words
6 March 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TORONTO -- Mining companies want young people to look at old rocks.
Despite the mining industry's continuing rough patch, few long-term issues worry executives as much as a
shortage of young geologists and engineers. It is helping to drive cost inflation of 10% a year at many
remote mining sites and has forced companies to rely even more on older geologists.
That is why mining companies paid for Sylvia Gumpesberger to spend her Tuesday morning at the annual
Prospectors and Developers Association of Canada conference here, flashing pictures of rapper Kanye
West's mouth to local ninth and 10th graders.
"Do we recognize Kanye by his smile?" she asked a table of teenagers. "How do you like those
diamond-encrusted teeth?
Bling was the thing. The kids were hooked, and Mrs. Gumpesberger launched into a lecture on carats,
diamond valuation, conflict minerals and mineral properties.
"There is a critical shortage of geologists," she said. "And it's vital that these kids understand that's a
possibility for them."
The professor of jewelry studies at George Brown College in Toronto was one of a dozen academics and
professionals addressing elementary and high-school students at this year's PDAC. The schools program,
organized by Mining Matters, an industry-funded group that says it has trained or educated 490,000
Canadian teachers, students and citizens since its inception in 1994, was conducted in a room decorated with
charts, rock samples and a bicycle with parts labeled by mineral or chemical ingredient.
"In Canada, we need 60 to 80,000 more geologists and engineers in the next 10 years," said Rohan Millar, a
45-year-old geologist for Vancouver-based Goldcorp Inc., a major gold producer, after he told a table about
his discovery last summer of $375 million worth of gold in northern Ontario. "These kids are who it's going to
be when I retire. We need to take away the illusion of everything that's bad about mining."
The kids listened keenly to Mr. Millar's tales of wandering the bush and his description of how he persuaded
the company's board to spend $9 million for exploratory drilling. They juggled the rock samples he handed
around.
But what about actually working in mining?
Luka Manzini, 14, said he was contemplating mining as a career but was leaning more toward mechanical
engineering after a visit to a DuPont & Co. plant. "Rocks aren't as cool as robots and blowing things up," he
said.
What might appeal to kids about working in mining? "Travel, money, being outdoors, going to see all these
amazing places," said the schools-program manager, Janice Williams.
"Money and travel would be fine," said 14-year-old Antonio Medeiros. He is thinking about becoming a mining
geologist. "It's more just to see how the Earth works," he explained.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics: Gold: Why Some Investors Are Still Hanging On
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics: Gold: Why Some Investors Are Still Hanging On
By Liam Pleven
852 words
2 March 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold no longer looks like a surefire bet. But many investors still are taking a shine to it as protection against
other wagers going bad.
Bullion prices have climbed in each of the past 12 years, shooting up nearly sixfold. Now, though, gold is in a
slump, down 6% so far this year and 12% since October. On Friday, it settled at $1,571.90 per troy ounce,
17% below the all-time high, not adjusted for inflation, of $1,880.70 hit in August 2011.
Some hedge funds and other large investors are dumping gold and hoping to profit if prices fall further.
Money managers held 62,000 futures contracts that would pay off if gold drops, more than twice as many as
at the end of 2012, according to government data released Friday.
Still, gold retains an allure for investors concerned about risks to the global economy, say advisers. Such
investors are holding the precious metal as a hedge against fiscal policy battles in Washington, continuing
credit woes in Europe and other persistent risks they fear could slam the value of other investments, though
even some proponents have trimmed back their allocation.
Gold has enjoyed "an amazing run," says Larry Hilzenrath, director of research at GenSpring Family Offices,
based in Jupiter, Fla., which acts as adviser on more than $15 billion in investments for wealthy clients.
"Everybody should be cautious about something that's had an incredible rise like that."
In January, GenSpring pared back its recommended gold allocation for some clients to 3.5% to 4% of their
portfolios, down from 5%, he says.
Gold remains a "decent diversifier" for investors, Mr. Hilzenrath says, particularly given that the problems
threatening financial markets aren't fully resolved. The firm's clients still hold about $230 million worth of
shares in SPDR Gold Trust, a bullion-backed exchange-traded fund.
In recent years, gold often has been seen as more of a speculative trade, especially for investors trying to
recover from steep losses suffered in the financial crisis. Its price moves at times perplexed traditionalists
who saw it as an investment that should rise when others fall, and vice versa.
Instead, investors often bid up gold at the same time as stocks or other risky investments, or discarded them
at the same time, in what became known as the "risk-on, risk-off" trade. From 2009 to 2012, gold rose in
tandem with the Dow Jones Industrial Average.
Last year marked the first time since 2003 that gold prices underperformed the Dow. This year, they have
headed in opposite directions. Share prices have continued their rally even as gold tumbles, sending the
blue-chip stock average up 7.5% this year, to near a record high.
That has boosted the stock holdings of investors such as Roy Steward, a Lutheran pastor who lives in
Altoona, Pa. But it hasn't dimmed his enthusiasm for gold as insurance for his portfolio. Gold he holds
through the Perth Mint in Australia represents about a fifth of his investments.
Mr. Steward wasn't dissuaded by recent news that billionaire George Soros's fund had reduced its holdings in
SPDR Gold Trust at the end of last year, compared with the previous quarter.
"We're of the mind it's time to get in and buy some more," Mr. Steward says.
Individual investors are a large part of gold's fan base, so their attitudes are important. According to State
Street Corp., which runs SPDR Gold Trust, institutional investors held 43% of the shares in the ETF as of
Dec. 31, near the low end of the range for institutional ownership since 2009, based on quarterly filings.

--- Page 127 ---
Page 127 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The risk, of course, is that gold still has much further to fall. In recent days, analysts at Goldman Sachs
Group and BNP Paribas both lowered their forecasts. "The fall in prices could end up being faster and larger
than we expect," Goldman Sachs said in a research note explaining the move.
Gold's last big decline lasted for decades. After peaking above $800 an ounce in 1980, prices fell sharply and
didn't climb back to that level for 27 years.
Yet there also are forces supporting gold prices, including continued bullion purchases by deep-pocketed
central banks trying to diversify their reserves, particularly in emerging markets. The banks have become net
buyers in recent years, giving prices a boost.
Gold's potential to serve as protection in another market downturn also remains on investors' minds. It gained
6% in 2008, when the Dow fell 34%.
"The most recent price action has not caused increased investor concern," says Jeff Marshall, managing
director of research at Convergent Wealth Advisors, based in Washington.
Convergent's clients typically keep about 2% to 4% of their money in gold, he says, and the firm hasn't
recommended changing the allocation recently. "We expect that we'll have a position in gold for some time,"
he says.

Debunking the Myths About Central Banks
Debunking the Myths About Central Banks
By Gerald P. O'Driscoll Jr.
1,162 words
28 February 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Myths govern modern central banking. Like many myths, they contain an element of truth that has been
distorted by exaggeration and misapplication. This year marks the 100th anniversary of the U.S. Federal
Reserve System -- an appropriate time for some long-overdue myth-busting.
The first myth is that central banks are intrinsically necessary for market economies. History and theory belie
this.
The Federal Reserve was not founded until 1913, and it had no monetary role. The U.S. operated under a
gold standard and had no need for a central bank to control the money supply. A gold, or any commodity,
standard places a natural limitation on money creation, which is the resource cost of extracting the
commodity. It is only with fiat (paper) money that central banks are necessary to control the money supply.
The Bank of Canada was not founded until 1935. The Canadian banking system survived the Great
Depression with no major bank failures. By contrast, thousands of U.S banks failed, despite the existence of
the Federal Reserve. These large-scale failures were ended by FDR's bank holiday, not by any Fed
contribution to banking stability.
The second myth is that central banks are needed as a lender of last resort -- that is, to supply liquidity in
times of financial stress when short-term lending freezes up. The Federal Reserve's lending in the aftermath
of Lehman's collapse in 2008 is the new textbook example of this function. But this argument has the
causality exactly backwards.
Walter Bagehot, the eminent 19th-century British economic journalist, coined the phrase "lender of last resort"
in his classic book, "Lombard Street." He recognized this was an essential function for the Bank of England.
However, the context is often dropped. Bagehot knew that a central bank inevitably resulted in a
concentration of reserves within that institution, making it the lender of last resort. But he did not believe that
a central bank was inevitable or desirable.
For Bagehot, "the natural system" was the one "which would have sprung up if Government had let banking
alone." There would have been "many banks of equal or not altogether unequal size." He described this as
"the many reserve system," in which each bank held reserves for itself, which he believed would have meant
a stronger banking system. In modern parlance, Bagehot's celebrated "lender of last resort" is a second-best
solution -- second to a world of competitive banks and no central bank.
In the post-Civil War era, the U.S. banking system did not operate as Bagehot's "natural system."
Government regulations concentrated bank reserves in major cities, with the result that the economy was
subject to panics and bank runs (which were rare in other countries), culminating in the Panic of 1907.
Instead of fixing the problems of the national banking system, however, lawmakers led by the progressive
president, Woodrow Wilson, created a central bank, the Federal Reserve System.
A third myth is that of central-bank independence. In the U.S., the Federal Reserve is viewed as having
gained independence as the result of the Accord of 1951 with the U.S. Treasury. After the accord, the Fed
was no longer required to maintain the prices of government bonds and thus fix interest rates. That
requirement, born of the fiscal needs of World War II, hindered the Fed from fighting inflation by preventing it
from raising interest rates during the Korean War.
Since 1951, there has been no relevant change in the Fed's legal status. The bank has acted independently
at times -- but at other times its actions have been anything but independent of the other branches of
government.
In the 1950s, under Fed Chairman William McChesney Martin, inflation remained low. Yet his had little to do
with Martin. President Eisenhower was an inflation hawk, and throughout the 1950s budget deficits were low

--- Page 131 ---
Page 131 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
or nonexistent. Once Presidents Kennedy and Johnson accepted Keynesian fiscal activism, deficits rose.
Martin was happy to accommodate the government with easy money. He did not believe monetary policy
could -- or should -- operate independently of fiscal policy. What followed was the first peacetime inflation in
U.S. history.
The Fed's independence hit a nadir under Chairman Arthur Burns. The diary he kept during the Nixon years
confirms that Fed policy became subservient to administration goals and the president's re-election
campaign. As he wrote in one diary entry, he told Nixon that "I was looking after monetary policy and he did
not need to be concerned about the possibility that the Federal Reserve would starve the economy." The
great inflation of the 1970s was the outcome.
Paul Volcker, chairman from 1979 to 1987, restored the Fed's inflation-fighting reputation, and the bank
under his management is held up as a model of independence. And true enough, there were many in the
legislative branch, as well as outside the government, who complained bitterly about his tight-money policy,
which ultimately reigned in inflation and spurred growth. Even so Mr. Volcker, like Martin before him, had
strong support from the two presidents during whose administrations he served, Jimmy Carter and Ronald
Reagan.
If we fast forward to today, it is difficult to portray the Fed under Chairman Ben Bernanke as operating
independently in any meaningful sense. In 2011, the Fed purchased an unprecedented 77% of Treasury
debt. With his long-term commitment to ultra-low interest rates, Mr. Bernanke has hitched monetary policy
to the fiscal policy of the Obama administration in a bid to inflate asset prices. That is the opposite of what is
supposed to be central bank independence -- and places the Fed closer to a presidential administration than
it has been since the days of Burns and Nixon.
The lesson from this history is what I call "central banking without romance," after a famous article by the late
Nobel prizewinning economist James M. Buchanan, "Politics Without Romance." A central bank is
necessary as long as an economy is wedded to a fiat currency. And it may at times behave independently --
but not in the face of large-scale budget deficits, as we have today.
Pursuit of price stability is the one goal that nearly everyone agrees is a central bank responsibility. Yet it is
the one on which the Fed and other central banks have failed miserably. Since the Fed's founding in 1913,
consumer prices have increased by a factor of 23 times. If the U.S. can extricate itself from fiscal deficits,
price stability would be an attainable goal for central banks. Otherwise, central banking is nothing but
mythology.
---
Mr. O'Driscoll is senior fellow at the Cato Institute, and was formerly vice president at the Federal Reserve
Bank of Dallas.
(See related letters: "Despite the Myths, the Federal Reserve Is Fairly Popular" -- WSJ March 6, 2013)
Subscribe to WSJ: http://online.wsj.com?mod=djnwires

A continental divide separated stock investors in Europe and the U.S. on Tuesday.
Across the Atlantic, stock markets tumbled as elections in Italy threatened to spark new political turmoil in the
region.
In the U.S., the Dow Jones Industrial Average had one of its best days this year. Having staged a selloff on

by the Federal Reserve on Tuesday.

weeks and more turmoil for financial markets.

"The reassurance that the Fed chairman tried to offer gave investors a pretext for kind of ignoring what's

course," Mr. McCain said.

fell to 1.877%, from 1.895% on Monday, as bond yields move inversely to prices. Gold jumped $29, or 1.8%,
to $1,615.20 a troy ounce, its largest one-day percentage gain since November. Gold tends to benefit from
easy-monetary policies, as investors seek the metal as a hedge against the inflation that may follow the
increased liquidity in the financial system.
Oil prices dropped 0.5% to settle at $92.63 a barrel. The dollar rose against the euro and yen.
Asian stocks were mixed early Wednesday. Japan's Nikkei was down 0.8%, as an upturn in the yen against
the dollar in Asian trading hurt stocks of Japanese exporters. But Hong Kong's Hang Seng index was up

was up 0.1%.

the Dow. Shares rose after the home-improvement retailer reported better-than-expected quarterly results,
announced a stock-buyback program and raised its quarterly dividend.

Why There Will Be No New Bretton Woods
Why There Will Be No New Bretton Woods
By Benn Steil
1,110 words
27 February 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
China, Japan and South Korea agree on little these days. Yet they seem increasingly united in the belief that
their economic sovereignty -- their ability to control inflows and outflows of capital, and the consequences for
their economies -- is hostage to imprudent policy innovations coming from the U.S. Federal Reserve.
In December, Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe observed critically that "Central banks around the world
are printing money. . . . America is the prime example. If it goes on like this, the yen will inevitably strengthen.
It's vital to resist this."
Speaking from Beijing in September, Bank of Korea Governor Kim Choong-soo said that "Korea and China
need to make concerted efforts to minimize the negative spillover effect arising from the monetary policies of
advanced nations," namely the United States.
The root of the problem, as People's Bank of China Governor Zho Xiaochuan explained in a November 2010
speech, is that whereas U.S. monetary policy "may be optimal for the U.S. alone . . . it is not necessarily
optimal for the world. There is a conflict between the U.S. dollar's domestic role and its international
settlement role."
This conflict is a leftover defect of "the Bretton Woods system," he said, referring to the historic 1944
conference that anointed the dollar a unique surrogate for gold at the foundation of the global monetary
system. By 1961, when the first nine European countries met the requirement that their currencies be
convertible into dollars, this system was already coming under strain owing to a deteriorating U.S. balance of
payments and corresponding loss of gold reserves. It collapsed 10 years later when President Nixon
suspended the dollar's convertibility into gold in order to prevent the depletion of America's gold stock. But a
lack of alternatives meant that the dollar remained the world's dominant reserve and trade currency, Mr. Zho
noted, despite "the frequency and increasing intensity of financial crises" after 1971.
In the wake of the 2008 financial crisis, pundits and world leaders alike called for a new Bretton Woods to
address the global financial imbalances that had become both dangerous and endemic. These imbalances
have been created predominantly by the U.S. and China, which together account for a third of world gross
domestic product.
Dollars sent to China for merchandise come back to the U.S. overnight in the form of low-interest loans and
are then recycled through the U.S. financial system to create more cheap credit. No force acts to reverse
Chinese trade surpluses or U.S. trade deficits -- there is no sustained dollar depreciation to make U.S. goods
more competitive, and no Fed tightening to restrain the growth of U.S. credit. The most dramatic effects of
this have been seen in the U.S. housing market, where a bubble created by low interest rates and easy
credit burst in 2007 with terrible effects on the global economy.
China has built up an astounding mountain of monetary reserves: $3.3 trillion, approximately 60% of which
comprises U.S. government securities. The U.S. has accumulated the world's largest international debt: $15.9
trillion. Each government eyes the trajectory of the two stockpiles with trepidation -- the Chinese fear a
collapse in the global purchasing power of their dollar hoard, the Americans a collapse in funding for their
debt. The U.S. accuses China of "manipulating" its currency, keeping it artificially low to stimulate exports and
discourage imports, while China blames U.S. profligacy and lax monetary controls.
China is, unlike the U.S. in the 1940s, in no position to orchestrate a Bretton Woods-type refashioning of the
global monetary architecture. The U.S. today is hardly the supplicant, war-torn Britain of the '40s, when the
U.S. Treasury forced it to remove the remaining trade and currency convertibility restrictions that sustained
the pound sterling's still-significant international role.
The U.S. still pays its bills in a currency it prints. Despite its large and growing debt, it has sold record new
issues of it at record low interest rates in a time of trans-Atlantic financial crisis. The dollar still accounts for

--- Page 135 ---
Page 135 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
60% of global foreign-exchange reserves (down from 70% a decade ago). Today 75% of global imports from
countries other than the U.S. are still denominated in dollars. At present, America has no need to
accommodate calls for it to sacrifice its exorbitant privilege to some vague vision of the global good.
China believes the U.S.-dominated international financial architecture fails to provide adequate security for its
economic interests, yet it can identify no alternative blueprint that doesn't imply massive financial losses on its
reserves, dislocation for its export industries, and potential social unrest.
The Bretton Woods saga unfurled at a unique crossroads in modern history. An ascendant anticolonial
superpower, the U.S., used its economic leverage over an insolvent allied imperial power, Great Britain, to set
the terms by which the latter would cede its dwindling dominion over the rules and norms of foreign trade and
finance. Britain cooperated only because the overriding aim of survival seemed to dictate the course. The
monetary architecture that the U.S. Treasury powered through a gathering of dollar-starved allies ultimately
fell of its own contradictions: There is no stable, durable circumstance in which the U.S. can emit enough
dollars to satisfy the world's trading needs and few enough to ensure that they can always be redeemed for a
fixed amount of gold.
Many hope that the International Monetary Fund, created at Bretton Woods, can be the catalyst for a new
cooperative monetary architecture. Yet history suggests that this won't occur until the U.S. and China come to
the conclusion that the consequences of muddling on, without the prospect of correcting the endemic
imbalances between them, are too great.
Meanwhile, trade tensions may grow much worse -- as they did in the 1930s, during the last great
international currency war. China's recent bilateral agreements with Japan, Brazil, Russia and Turkey to
pursue trade without dollars could be a worrisome harbinger, insofar as each would be more likely to
undertake global trade discrimination in order to balance its bilateral trade than to stockpile other currencies.
The U.S. had sought to eliminate this damaging stratagem permanently at Bretton Woods; it may soon
re-emerge.
---
Mr. Steil is director of international economics at the Council on Foreign Relations and author of "The Battle
of Bretton Woods," just out from Princeton University Press.

Investors Size Up Risk's Resurgence --- Dow Tumbles 216 As Vote in Italy, Capitol Impasse Spook Investors
Monday's Markets
Investors Size Up Risk's Resurgence --- Dow Tumbles 216 As Vote in Italy, Capitol Impasse Spook
Investors
By Jonathan Cheng and Carolyn Cui
947 words
26 February 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After taking a back seat for weeks, two market demons, Europe and Washington, showed they still had power
to haunt investors.
Inconclusive election results in Italy sparked fears of political instability that could unsettle the wider region.
And in the U.S., political leaders appeared likely to allow potentially damaging spending cuts to kick in. Both
events on Monday came on top of concerns that the Federal Reserve may end its bond-buying program
earlier than planned.
All three combined for the worst day for stocks in nearly four months, a rare banner day for gold, and a
strong rally in Treasurys.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average slumped 216.40 points, or 1.5%, to 13784.17, its biggest one-day point
and percentage drop since Nov. 7. The blue-chip average now sits at its lowest level in a month. Of the
stocks in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, just 3% achieved a gain.
Gold, which had declined more than 7% over the past month, notched its biggest one-day rise this month,
bouncing back 0.9%, to $1,586.20 a troy ounce. The 10-year Treasury note strung together its fourth day of
gains, driving its yield down to 1.895% late Monday, a level unseen in more than a month, as bond yields
move inversely to prices.
In a sign of rising market concerns, the CBOE Market Volatility Index, the "fear gauge" known as the VIX,
soared 34% to its highest level this year. The last time the VIX rose more than 30% in a day was during
2011's debt-ceiling debate.
The reaction was notable because investors had to a large extent dismissed macroeconomic worries in
recent weeks. The Dow was up 6.8% this year before Monday, nearing its record, but the gains have been
enough to give some investors pause.
Some investors suggested that Italy's political instability could undermine the euro zone's third-largest
economy, which could ripple to the bloc's other countries.
"There's been a gradual turn in the news flow away from the positive we've been seeing at the beginning of
this year," said Alan Gayle, senior investment strategist for Atlanta-based RidgeWorth Investments, which
manages about $48 billion in assets. "The news flow has not been good to begin with, and the final straw
came from the Italian elections."
In Italy, voters endorsed parties that had promised to tone down or reverse the financial- austerity plan Italy
has promised its European partners, giving surprise lifts to both the center-right coalition of former Premier
Silvio Berlusconi and a party of protest led by a former comedian.
Late Monday, the left-wing coalition led by the Democratic Party appeared to have gained razor-thin victories
in the Senate and lower house but without the votes needed to pass legislation.
One trader described the election as a "disaster," echoing the sentiment of other investors, because the
muddled outcome could bring uncertainty for months.
At the same time, the U.S. government appears to be barreling toward a set of automatic budget cuts known
as the "sequester," which would kick in on Friday, reducing government spending by $1.2 trillion over the
course of a decade.

--- Page 139 ---
Page 139 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Many investors also are now turning their focus to the second half of this week, which will see a barrage of
economic data on the U.S., including the monthly jobs report, as well as congressional testimony by Fed
Chairman Ben Bernanke.
Prices at the gas pump also are at historically high levels for this time of year, raising concerns that
consumers could feel their budgets pinched.
In the U.S., stocks initially jumped on Monday amid expectations Italian voters would favor a government that
would continue austerity programs. The Dow rose as much as 81 points, less than 100 points short of its
record close, before the tone soured.
"The election complicates some of the steady progress we had seen on the political front in Europe," Mr.
Gayle said. "Italy had been moving along in a much more disciplined way, cutting back government spending,
and the austerity measures had soothed some of the market anxieties. Berlusconi is viewed as part of the
problem, not a part of the solution."
Among stocks, Lowe's fell 4.8% after the home-improvement retailer's earnings forecast trailed analysts'
estimates.
Barnes & Noble surged 11% after Leonard Riggio, the company's chairman and largest shareholder,
confirmed he has an interest in buying out the retailer's bookstores.
Meanwhile, some investors anticipated selling pressure would carry over into European trading Tuesday.
That would put the focus on Mr. Bernanke, who heads to Capitol Hill to face questions from legislators over
the central bank's commitment to supporting the economy through bond purchases.
On Monday, Italy's FTSE MIB index pared an intraday rise of 4%, briefly turning negative before finishing with
a gain of 0.7%. The Stoxx Europe 600 finished down 0.1%, while the euro tumbled 1% against the dollar, to
$1.3062, its worst day since Jan. 3. The euro has fallen 3.8% this month against the greenback.
The selloff continued in Asia early Tuesday. Japan's Nikkei was down down 1.6%, Hong Kong's Hang Seng
index and Australia's S&P/ASX 200 were down 0.7%, the Shanghai composite was down 0.5% and South
Korea's Kospi was down 0.4%.

Investors Size Up Risk's Resurgence --- Gold Jumps After Recent Declines; Paulson Is Believer
Commodities Report
Investors Size Up Risk's Resurgence --- Gold Jumps After Recent Declines; Paulson Is Believer
By Gregory Zuckerman and Matt Day
1,085 words
26 February 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
John Paulson still believes in gold.
That has been a painful stance lately. But Monday finally brought some good news for the well-known
hedge-fund manager and other gold bulls.
Gold jumped as high as $1,596 a troy ounce in electronic trading, after settling up $13.80, or 0.9%, at
$1,586.20. Concerns about elections in Italy and big spending cuts in the U.S. helped drive the rally.
It was a contrast to the declines seen over the past few months, as an absence of worrisome news helped
damp enthusiasm for gold. Prices have fallen 5.3% this year and are down 16% from a record closing high of
$1,888.70 set in August 2011. The final three months of 2012 were the metal's worst quarter since the 2008
financial crisis.
While many investors remain skeptical about the long-term prospects for gold, some large hedge funds are
beginning to pay a bit more attention as the price has slid.
Behind the troubles for gold have been an improving global economy and stock market, which have left
investors searching for riskier investments, such as stocks, and selling safe-harbor investments, like gold.
Meanwhile, some who bought gold as a hedge against inflation have dumped the metal because consumer
prices have been tame. Others say stocks will do at least as well as gold if inflation rises.
The dollar also has held up, which reduces gold's allure. And interest rates have moved higher, making it
more costly to hold gold, which earns no return. Gold-mining stocks have been even more disappointing,
partly because of management flubs and poor cash flow in recent years.
Despite all the gold negativity, Mr. Paulson's firm, Paulson & Co., remains among the biggest fans and is
holding on to much of its gold stash. Paulson continues to hold the largest position in the SPDR Gold Trust
exchange-traded fund, or GLD, with almost 22 million shares, a position he didn't change last quarter. The
firm's large positions in gold miners AngloGold Ashanti Ltd., Detour Gold Corp., NovaGold Resources Inc.
and Barrick Gold Corp. were barely changed last quarter, according to filings.
It isn't clear if Mr. Paulson has adjusted his gold-derivative positions, which are considered large. But Mr.
Paulson has told clients he believes gold will rise over the long term, as central banks expand their money
supplies and inflation eventually picks up. He hasn't, however, been making a new push into gold, his
investors say.
A few of Mr. Paulson's colleagues on Wall Street agree with this stance. Hedge fund Och-Ziff Capital
Management Group LLC added to its GLD position in the fourth quarter, but the firm has less than 1% of its
overall holdings in the ETF, according to FactSet.
Vishaal Bhuyan, who runs $20 million hedge fund Nariman Point LLC, says he has been buying physical
gold "on dips," or as gold prices dipped recently, on a bet that interest rates won't soar.
"If you look at gold as protection, you need to own it in coins or bars and not an ETF," says Mr. Bhuyan, who
is holding gold as a long-term investment but acknowledges prices could fall to $1,350 an ounce.
The bulls are in the clear minority lately, though. Money managers tracked by the Commodity Futures Trading
Commission this month cut their bullish bets on gold futures and options to the lowest level in more than four
years. Funds slashed their net bullish bets by 32% during the week ended Feb. 19 to about 42,000 contracts,
the lowest November 2008.

--- Page 141 ---
Page 141 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Big-name hedge funds and other traders have turned away from gold in recent months, and some say they
are staying away.
Steve Mandel's Lone Pine Capital LLC dumped its entire position in the GLD ETF, in the fourth quarter, or
nearly 2.6 million shares, according to securities filings. Other well-respected hedge funds, including Moore
Capital Management LP and Scout Capital Management LLC, also sold all their positions in GLD in the
quarter, according to filings. Representatives of the firms declined to comment.
"Gold has been a momentum trade and people are getting bored with it," says the head of a major hedge
fund.
George Soros's $24 billion Soros Fund Management pulled about $100 million from its holdings in GLD in the
fourth quarter, or roughly half of its position. The move, however, didn't reflect a souring on gold, according to
people close to the firm, which in the past has used a variety of investments and derivatives to buy and sell
gold.
Mr. Paulson made his reputation by scoring about $20 billion in profits betting against subprime mortgages.
He padded those gains by buying gold in early 2009, well before it began to rally. Mr. Paulson began a
dedicated gold hedge fund, which manages about $1 billion, to wager on gold.
The gold weakness has cost Mr. Paulson. His own money represents more than half the gold fund,
according to investors. And Mr. Paulson has told clients more than half of his personal wealth is in his firm's
gold-denominated funds.
In the options market, bearish sentiment has been on the rise. In the past month, the average ratio of trading
in bearish options relative to bullish options has risen to its highest level since October 2011, according to
options-data firm Trade Alert.
Some large hedge funds say they have become more interested in gold as it has slid and sentiment soured,
but they are waiting for a sharper downturn before doing any serious buying.
Daniel Arbess, who manages the PWP Xerion hedge fund, bought physical gold in late 2008 but sold his
investment last year. Xerion is avoiding gold in anticipation that central banks may eventually need to ease
off their monetary policy, driving rates higher.
"We don't want to be in gold ahead of the normalization" of rates, Mr. Arbess says, though he may come
back into gold at a later date as a hedge against inflation as the Fed sells some of the bonds it has
purchased.

Stocks Get Back In Black
Friday's Markets
Stocks Get Back In Black
By Matt Jarzemsky
626 words

The Wall Street Journal

English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Dow industrials posted their third triple-digit point gain of the year, as Hewlett-Packard and American
International Group rallied on earnings and a reading on German business confidence topped expectations.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average advanced 119.95 points, or 0.9%, to 14000.57, reversing most of a
155-point slide the prior two days.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index tacked on 13.18 points, or 0.9%, to 1515.60. The Nasdaq Composite
Index rose 30.33 points, or 1%, to 3161.82.
"Any selloff we get is going to be shallow," said Scott Wren, senior equity strategist at Wells Fargo Advisors in
St. Louis, which manages $1.2 trillion. "The mentality is, 'I want to wait for the market to pull back so I can buy
stocks at lower levels.' The Fed's on our side, companies are making money and the growth environment is
OK."
Hewlett-Packard gained $2.10, or 12%, to $19.20, leading the Dow higher, after the technology company late
Thursday reported a quarterly profit that topped analysts' expectations and gave a better-than-expected
earnings forecast.
AIG rose 1.17, or 3.1%, to 38.45, after saying late Thursday that it swung to a loss, but operating profit in
some segments exceeded analyst expectations.
For the week, the Dow finished with a 0.1% gain, while the S&P 500 slipped 0.3% to snap a seven-week
streak of weekly advances.
Weighing on the benchmarks this week, minutes from the Federal Reserve's latest policy meeting released
Wednesday showed some officials were concerned about the consequences of low-interest-rate policies
meant to help revive the economy. That sparked U.S. stocks' biggest two-day selloff of the year, as such
policies have been a boon to stocks. But strategists said the pullback also set the stage for Friday's rebound.
European markets rose, with the Stoxx Europe 600 up 1.3% to recover most of the losses from the previous
session, after data showed German business confidence increased much more than expected in February.
The index advanced 0.4% on the week.
Germany's DAX index climbed 1% and gained 0.9% for the week. The measure is up 0.7% this year.
"From the U.S. perspective, Germany is a very industrial and export-oriented economy; If they're seeing
optimism on their order books, then there's reason to be optimistic globally," said Wasif Latif, vice president of
equity investments at USAA Investments in San Antonio. His firm manages $54 billion.
Italy's FTSE MIB index advanced 1.4%, after falling to a two-month low on Thursday, ahead of general
elections in Italy this weekend. Some investors fear a new government may not continue the country's
reformist path.
In Asia, China's Shanghai Composite Index fell 0.5% to a one-month low and declined 4.9% on the week.
Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index also shed 0.5%, dropping 2.8% for the week, to near a two-month low.
Investors are concerned that policy makers will implement additional tightening measures to control
residential property prices.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average gained 0.7% and climbed 1.9% on the week. The index has risen 14 of the
past 15 weeks and has climbed 9.5% this year. Australia's S&P/ASX 200 added 0.8%, rebounding from
losses on Thursday, but fell 0.3% on the week, snapping a five-week winning streak.

OFF DUTY --- Eating & Drinking -- Game-Changing Meal: Ain't No Mountain Haute Enough --- The hottest table in France right now is tucked...
OFF DUTY --- Eating & Drinking -- Game-Changing Meal: Ain't No Mountain Haute Enough --- The
hottest table in France right now is tucked away in the snowy Alpine village of Courchevel
By Alexander Lobrano
2,001 words
23 February 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
D4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Just before Christmas, a single meal on a cold mountain completely upended my ideas about what winter
food can be. As a native New Englander and longtime Parisian, I thought I knew all about the joys of
cold-weather cooking -- solid, solacing, maternal -- but over the course of this three-hour feed I discovered
that it can be light, surprising and even subliminally erotic.
I was tired at the end of the long ascending journey that brought me from Paris to Courchevel, the most
glamorous of France's Alpine resorts, and the temple-like, almost-all-white dining room of Le 1947 restaurant
in the hotel Cheval Blanc. I was also very hungry. But as a veteran reviewer of the modernist cuisine that's
been giving off a lot of hot air in Europe for over 20 years now, I wondered if I was in for more of the same --
that is, if I'd really be fed. "The agony and the ecstasy" was how chef Yannick Alleno, 44, had described the
"intensive gastronomic research" he and his team had been doing in a Paris kitchen-cum-laboratory during Le
1947's annual closure from early-April to mid-December. Would the new menu they'd created be mere
amusing smoke and mirrors? Would I be reading a room-service menu at 2 a.m.?
The restaurant's name refers to the most famous vintage of wine produced by Cheval Blanc, the Bordelais
chateau that's owned, like the hotel, by LVMH, the French luxury-goods conglomerate. Le 1947 has two
Michelin stars, and I've known Mr. Alleno's often-brilliant cooking for many years. He was chef at Le Meurice,
where he won three Michelin stars, from 2003 until he resigned in December. As I sat down to dinner at Le
1947 that evening, I knew I was in for high culinary art, but I was hoping for something more along the lines of
Pieter Bruegel's "Hunters in the Snow" than anything by Dali. Over the many courses that followed, though, I
got tastes of both medieval naturalism and 21st-century showmanship.
I was snug in the snowy pile of the sheepskin draped over my chair, savoring the way a first sip of Krug
Grande Cuvee always unlaces a nerve or two, when a waiter arrived. "Bonsoir et bienvenue," he said as he
lowered before me a teardrop-shape porcelain bowl trailing the potent perfume of duck consomme. Tiny
amber beads of fat glistened on the surface of a ruddy broth that glinted with flecks of gold leaf. I was struck
by the layers of flavor: duck, celery root, foie gras and more duck. The prim but perfect counterpoint to all this
richness came from a single sprig of minty Alpine pimpernel.
Next up, a suite of intricately wrought hors d'oeuvres. There was a dish of rice cream, shrimp and sake gelee
sprinkled with rice vinegar, followed by a langoustine mousse tartlette with a glossy black veil of Osetra
caviar, and grilled scallops with shavings of black truffle and microscopic fronds of watercress. One of these
hors d'oeuvres, a tiny shell of crunchy duck skin filled with pickled turnips and a chutney of seaweed, offered,
I later realized, a teasing preview of the themes in the dishes to follow: the revelation of a complex spectrum
of tastes within a single ingredient; the sensuality of contrasting textures; the anarchic pleasure of unexpected
punctuation.
If all of this sounds like a schuss downhill from the fondue and raclette traditionally associated with Alpine
eating, it's because Courchevel has emerged as a sort of Saint-Tropez of the snow. An opulent winter resort
where people come as much to eat these days as they do to ski, this tiny village has five Michelin two-star
restaurants, making it one of France's most exalted gastronomic destinations. Still, if Mr. Alleno's menus draw
on the finest produce from all over the country, he takes pride in using as many local ingredients as possible,
including omble chevalier, a superb fish from Lake Geneva, as well as Savoyard apples, herbs and
vegetables.
What remained consistent throughout the meal was a basso profondo depth of flavor. Every dish tasted so
vividly of what it was that I had the impression I'd never eaten any of it before. Cauliflower, for example: I was
served the stalks but not the florets -- because, as the waiter explained, the stalks have the most taste --
smothered in a beurre noisette (brown butter) and garnished with sliced black truffles. Or venison, a baby
loin, oven-roasted with oak logs and served with an almost feral poivrade sauce and a crunchy potato and
truffle galette.

--- Page 145 ---
Page 145 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
I'd spoken to the chef in the fall, as he was wrapping up menu development in Paris; I knew, going into this
meal, that one of Mr. Alleno's self-imposed challenges was to invent a new winter kitchen without relying on
too much fat for flavor. Instead, there was the constantly shifting contrast of textures and tones of acidity, a
perfect example being a vegetarian boudin noir (blood sausage) made with cubed beets and black rice and
garnished with fine slices of 25-year-old pickled Iranian garlic and caramelized apple. Surprises, like
tagliatelle made from dried egg yolks and nothing else in a sauce of chicken fat and minced black truffles,
kept the long winter's meal lively and also profoundly satisfying. Would that every three-hour dinner could
pass in the presence of the kind of intimate service made possible by a ratio of 12 staff to a maximum of 25
covers nightly. Gastronomic haute couture, bien sur.
"The restaurant as we know it today is finished," said Mr. Alleno. "In France, we've been following the dictates
of [the influential restaurant guides] Gault et Millau for two generations now, and we urgently need to
change." Mr. Alleno was referring to the miniaturization and minimalism of La Nouvelle Cuisine, which
regarded the empty dinner plate as the canvas for an artfully composed still-life. "The challenge is to
construct a meal where the taste buds are continuously tempted and excited but which moves toward a point
of real satiation." In other words, Mr. Alleno thinks drawn-out tasting menus don't make most people happy
unless there's some sort of sink-your-teeth-into-it moment that signals the main event -- in the case of my
meal at Le 1947, it was the venison with a poivrade sauce. "Like most middle-class Frenchmen," Mr. Alleno
said, "I believe all meals need a real plat" or main course.
Still, the central question of his research is more or less the same one that launched La Nouvelle Cuisine in
the 1960s: How to create taste without relying on excessive fat? Back then the solution was bright herbs and
jus, or super-reduced sauces made from bouillon skimmed of all fat; today, the answer is with super-slow,
low-temperature cooking and intensely concentrated flavor essences made possible by a new generation of
technology. Cooked sous-vide, or in a plastic pouch at a very low temperature, Mr. Alleno's sublime
cauliflower had the texture of a thick panna cotta because it had lost none of its moisture. Similarly, the
venison cooked in a superheated oven with burning oak logs was both seared and smoked so that it lost
none of its flavorful juices. Alas, I don't have sous-vide equipment or a wood-burning oven at home in Paris,
but this meal reminded me that my job in the kitchen is actually pretty straightforward. To wit, the purpose of
every technique and seasoning should be to make the produce you're cooking with as eloquent as possible.
Truth be told, I'd originally listened to Mr. Alleno rant about the sclerosis of French gastronomy with a certain
friendly skepticism: French chefs are always wanting to storm the Bastille and start all over again. But Mr.
Alleno really has pulled off a winter revolution in plummy, plutocratic Courchevel. As this ongoing winter
commitment occupies only three months of his year, and he's no longer cooking at the Hotel Meurice, I can't
wait to see what he'll to get up to in spring, summer and fall.
The Lowdown: Prix fixe dinner at Le 1947 costs 250 euros ($332). Cheval Blanc Hotel, Le Jardin Alpin,
Courchevel, chevalblanc.com
---
Bring the Flavors Home
It may ruffle feathers in my New England family, but Mr. Alleno's take on clams-on-the-half-shell was the most
spectacular encounter I've ever had with this bivalve. The recipe looks fussy at first glance, but in fact it's not
at all. It's also a perfect expression of what makes Mr. Alleno's cooking so fascinating. Everything the chef
does in the kitchen is intended to intensify the flavors of the produce he works with. Here, butter amplifies the
meatiness of the clams, while a dab of bracing citrus-and-kombu jelly highlights their iodine edge and a pinch
of black pepper, their sweetness. Crumbled Melba toast adds a bit of sandy texture -- a witty touch. This
recipe makes far more yuzu butter than you'll need -- each clam gets just a few scant shavings -- but you can
use the remainder as a dip for radishes, a schmear for toast or the base for a butter-and-white-wine sauce for
steamed shellfish.
-- A.L.
---
Clams, Black Pepper and Yuzu
TOTAL TIME: 1 hour
SERVES: 4
1 cup salted butter at room temperature
2 yuzu or 1 lemon

--- Page 146 ---
Page 146 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
4 cups sea salt, plus more to taste
3 cups cold water
1/2 ounce (one 4-by-7-inch sheet)
kombu (dried kelp)
1/2 cup dried bonito flakes
1 teaspoon ponzu sauce
1 teaspoon powdered gelatin
1 thin slice whole-wheat bread, frozen
2 pounds medium-size clams, such as Manila or Littleneck
Freshly ground black pepper
1. Make yuzu butter: In a medium bowl, use a fork to cream butter. Add juice and zest of 1 yuzu (or 1/2
lemon) and salt to taste, and mix well. Spoon mixture onto a piece of plastic wrap, roll into a tightly wrapped
cylinder and twist ends. Refrigerate until firm, 1 hour.
2. Meanwhile, make dashi stock and yuzu jelly: In a large pot over high heat, bring cold water and kombu just
to a boil. Immediately remove from heat and discard kombu. Add bonito flakes to pot and stir, then let stand 3
minutes. Pour dashi through a sieve lined with cheesecloth or paper towel into a bowl. In a small metal bowl,
combine 1/2 cup dashi stock, juice of 1 yuzu (or 1/2 lemon), ponzu and salt to taste. Add gelatin and stir to
dissolve. Set bowl in a larger bowl filled with ice cubes, place in refrigerator and let sit until gelatin softly sets,
30 minutes.
3. Make Melba toast: Preheat oven to 350 degrees. Put bread slice between two sheets of parchment paper
and flatten with a rolling pin until thin and crumbled. Pick up bottom layer of parchment along with bread,
place on a baking tray and bake 9 minutes. Let cool 10 minutes.
4. Prepare clams: Open clams and remove meat, reserving shells. Slice clams in half lengthwise.
5. To serve: Place 1 cup salt in each of four shallow bowls. Place 3 clam shells in each bowl, add 3 clam
halves to each shell and sprinkle with pepper. Use a vegetable peeler to shave cold yuzu butter chips into
yuzu jelly and top each clam with a dab of this mixture. Sprinkle a pinch of crumbled Melba toast over each
clam.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics: This Week

The Wall Street Journal

Markets Test Highs
Stocks touched five-year highs on Tuesday, then retreated after minutes from the last Federal Reserve
meeting were released. The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 0.1% to 14000.57, while the Standard &
Poor's 500 ended at 1515.60, down 0.3% from last week.
Older and More in Debt
U.S. families headed by those 75 or older saw their debt rise over a three-year period, according to a new
report by the Employee Benefit Research Institute. The average debt level increased to $27,409 in 2010 --
the most recent period studied -- for those families, up from $13,665 in 2007.
Midas, Touched
Gold, used by many investors as a hedge against economic turmoil, hit a seven-month low, falling below
$1,600 a troy ounce. Interest in the metal has weakened as U.S. equities have climbed to multiyear highs, as
China resumes growth and as Europe's debt crisis seems to be in a quiet period.
Hitting the Gas
The average U.S. price for regular unleaded gasoline hit $3.78 on Thursday, up 47 cents in the past month,
fueled in part by traders betting on stronger demand. The rise is a reversal from late 2012, when seasonally
adjusted energy prices fell, helping to keep inflation in check.
CFPB Probes Loans
The Consumer Financial Protection Bureau announced it is analyzing private student loans, hoping to
develop policy options to help borrowers manage debt. The agency is examining how borrowers deal with
student-loan payments and will examine alternate payment programs.
Housing Starts and Stops
New residential construction fell 8.5% in January after surging 15.7% in December. Yet many economists
continue to believe the housing sector is on track to recover and attribute the dip in new-home construction to
volatility in the apartment construction sector.

Gold Falls 2.3% in Week to Seven-Month Low
By Tatyana Shumsky
432 words

The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold prices ended at a seven-month low, resuming their downward march, as some investors opted to leave
the market amid dissatisfaction with the yellow metal's performance.
Gold for February delivery, the front-month contract, fell $5.80, or 0.4%, to $1,572.40 a troy ounce on the
Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, the lowest settlement price since July 18. The
precious metal is down 6.1% this year.
Gold slid 2.3% this past week and is down 12% from its 52-week high of $1,794.10 hit in October. Gold's fall
has come amid increased competition from better-performing stocks and as investors worry about the exit of
large fund managers like George Soros from the bullion market.
Gold prices made a move below the $1,600 level after a bearish reading of the Federal Reserve's January
meeting minutes was released Wednesday. Fed officials showed discomfort with existing monetary policy,
sparking worries that the central bank may tighten its easy-money measures sooner than expected.
"Market participants fear that the punch bowl is going to be removed a lot sooner than anticipated . . . that
detracts from wanting to buy gold as an inflationary hedge," said Matt Zeman, head of trading at Kingsview
Financial.
Mr. Zeman said $1,523 is the next level of support for gold, though the market could witness a temporary
rally or "dead cat bounce" this coming week before prices resume their march lower. "Gold has a lot of things
working against it right now," he said.
Brokers and analysts said that easing fears with regard to Europe's future added to the pressure on bullion
prices. Gold is widely considered a haven from economic uncertainty and currency risk, but demand for such
assets tends to wane when investors' fears subside.
"One of the things that drove a lot of people into gold is fear of the European Union breaking up and the euro
disintegrating. None of that happened," said Frank Lesh, broker and futures analyst with FuturePath Trading.
"Some of the reasons you'd buy and hold gold are just not there anymore," he said.
In a new twist, a record number of speculative investors is betting on lower gold prices through short futures
positions on the Comex. These bearish positions are adding to the weight on gold prices, brokers and
analysts said. "We've got people getting short gold, and rightfully so," Mr. Lesh said.

When Interest Rates Rise, Watch Out
When Interest Rates Rise, Watch Out
By Andy Kessler
1,197 words
22 February 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A11
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Can Ben Bernanke fly us through a needle's eye? Minutes released this week from the last Federal Reserve
policy meeting suggest evaluations are taking place that "might well lead the Committee to taper or end its
purchases before it judged that a substantial improvement in the outlook for the labor market had occurred."
It's about time. The experiment to kick-start the economy with near-zero interest rates has failed. Maybe our
central bankers have figured out that low rates are what is holding back lending and hiring and growth.
Meanwhile, even as the stock market hits highs not seen since 2007, everyone on Wall Street knows interest
rates will go up -- although no one except Mr. Bernanke knows when. Investors are playing a game of
chicken with rates, enjoying the ride but bracing for a downturn when the rates turn up. When rates go up,
bonds become more attractive than stocks because you get returns with less risk.
Those of us on Wall Street in 1994 witnessed something very similar. After several years of essentially flat
short-term rates, the Fed raised rates by 25 basis points (or 0.25%) on Feb. 4, 1994. The Fed raised
short-term rates a total of six times and 2.5% over the next 10 months.
In what became known as a bond-market massacre, the price of long bonds dropped almost 9%. The stock
market dropped 9% in three months, killing a then-vibrant market for initial public offerings.
Today we are at the bitter end of a three-decade-long interest rate cycle, culminating in Mr. Bernanke's
near-zero rates. You can't fall off the floor. And the prospect of higher interest rates is like the Sword of
Damocles hanging over the stock market.
On May 13, 1981, the three-month Treasury bill rate was 17.01% and the Dow Industrials were just under
1000. Today, three-month rates are under 0.12% and the Dow is headed south from 14000. What should
interest rates be? In January, the Consumer Price Index rose 1.6% year over year. In a normal economy
with a normal Fed, short rates should be 2%-3%.
Investors beware: The unwind is going to be bumpy. The entire U.S. bond market is over $30 trillion. Any
sudden increase in interest rates means current holdings would be worth less and might create turmoil from
runs or even "breaking the buck" at money-market funds. Gold is already down over 15% from last year's
October peak, anticipating this Fed move.
All along, Mr. Bernanke's intent was to allow banks to recapitalize despite all the toxic mortgage debt that he
and Treasury Secretary Timothy Geithner left on their balance sheets, so we've lived with almost four years of
sub-0.2% interest rates. The results of the latest bank stress tests are due out on March 7, but 15 of the 19
largest banks (Citigroup the glaring exception) passed the last one. The justification for low interest rates is
no longer to save banks, but instead to goose the stock market as an indirect way to create jobs.
Mr. Bernanke told CNBC last year that, "Our policies have contributed to a stronger stock market just as they
did in March 2009 when we did the first iteration of this program." No kidding. But as a policy tool, low
interest rates are like using gasoline to light your charcoal grill. You may singe your eyebrows, but it will
certainly light the coals. The question is will the coals stay lit when the gasoline burns off.
When rates rise, the bond market will sell off, but foreign investors seeking a safe haven from their messes
may continue to pile in and limit the damage, especially if incoming Treasury Secretary Jack Lew implements
rather than jawbones a strong dollar policy.
The stock market is another thing. Every day this trading venue captures collective expectations in prices --
expectations of corporate profits and interest rates and risk. Stocks are nothing more than all the future
earnings of a company discounted back to today. The discount rate is a combination of prevailing interest
rates plus some factor of risk.

--- Page 150 ---
Page 150 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But there is no risk or discount number published anywhere. It's touchy-feely. Expectations change daily. That
is why stocks are said to climb a wall of worry. When no one is worried, you run out of buyers and markets
top. When everyone thought Apple's share price was going to hit $1,000, and every hedge fund owned it, of
course it sold off as all that good sentiment was in the stock.
But there is one real market absolute: interest rates. Right now, mutual- and hedge-fund managers are
scrambling their brains trying to figure out when rates will rise, trying to outguess the Fed, other investors and
probably themselves. The economy dropped a tenth of a point last quarter, Spain and Portugal are still a
mess, only 157,000 jobs were created in the U.S. in January, tax rates are popping and corporate earnings
may grow only 1%-2% this year.
But if and as these worries ease, the Fed's Sword of Damocles will swing. The stock market is not going to
like it one bit. But why should the Fed care? Banks are recapitalized and the charcoals are lit. Our central
bankers should be agnostic to stock prices anyway. This doesn't mean the stock market is going to crash,
though dropping 1,000 points in a few weeks would not be surprising.
There is a possibility that Mr. Bernanke will thread the needle and manage to gradually raise rates, grow the
economy and keep the stock-market rally intact. Investors should be rooting for him. The Fed, when it does
move, is going to be slow and steady. Dallas Fed President Richard Fisher told Bloomberg radio recently, "I
don't want to go from wild turkey to cold turkey."
Rising rates are OK as long as corporate profits and the dollar are rising faster, especially if declining energy
and food prices provide a boost. If I were Mr. Bernanke, I wouldn't wait. Tomorrow morning I'd start raising
rates as a signal to all that the economy is on sound footing.
Announce loud and clear that you are going to raise the federal-funds rate 10 basis points (or 0.1%) every
month, like clockwork, until it exceeds the inflation rate, and then declare victory. The stock market might
even rally on the certainty and return to normalcy.
Rising rates are inevitable. But be advised that in selloffs, stocks fall into the valley of despair. The 1995-2000
bull run followed the bond-market massacre.
---
Mr. Kessler, a former hedge-fund manager, is the author most recently of "Eat People" (Portfolio, 2011).
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Interest Rates and Faith in the Fed" -- WSJ Feb. 28, 2013)
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: The Interest-Rate Sequester to Come" -- WSJ March 4, 2013)

month that the central bank's easy-money policies could lead to instability in financial markets, and might be
